Ruling of a Mouse Falling into a Well: Obligatory Removal and Purification
Matn and hashiya on ruling for mouse in well, water removal and purification of tools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for when a mouse falls into a well, identify the evidence from the hadith of Anas, and distinguish between the obligatory and recommended amounts of water removal.

Core Idea
Twenty buckets are wajib for a mouse in a well; increase to thirty is mustahabb — the command is zanni, not qat'i.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses the purification of a well when a small animal, like a mouse, dies in it. You will learn the obligatory amount of water to remove, the hadith proof, and how the Hanafi school handles related tools and varying numbers of animals. The text also records an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the amounts for multiple mice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
A mouse (or similar small animal) dies in a well without bloating; the ruling is wajib to remove twenty buckets after extraction.
The hukm level is wajib, not fard, because the dalil is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Anas ibn Malik: 'If a mouse falls into the well and is taken out immediately, twenty buckets are removed.'
The exact text is not quoted in full in the source but referenced; we rely on the transmission.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative form 'ينزح' (yuzahu) indicates obligation (wujub) according to Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub.
Since the hadith is ahad, the ruling is wajib, not fard.
4
Recognize the recommended increase
A variant narration includes doubt about thirty buckets; the Hanafi scholars treat the certain twenty as wajib and the additional ten as mustahabb (recommended) to preserve the transmitted wording.
The doubt is resolved by taking the lesser amount as certain.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf graded mice by number: four mice = one atonement, five to nine = chicken, ten = sheep. Muhammad graded by size intervals: three to five mice = cat, six = dog. Muhammad's view is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) and mufta bihi.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the practical amount of water to remove.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة أبي سعيد الخدري في الدجاجة وما قاربها يعطي حكمها، وتستحب الزيادة إلى خمسين، أو ستين لما روي عن عطاء والشعبي وإن مات فيها فأرة) بالهمز (أو نحوها) كعصفور، ولم ينتفخ لزم نزح عشرين دلوا) بعد إخراجه لقول أنس رضي الله عنه في فأرة ماتت في البئر وأخرجت من ساعتها ينزح عشرون ،دلواً، ويستحب الزيادة إلى ثلاثين لاحتمال زيادة الدلو المذكور في الأثر على ما قدر به من الوسط (وكان ذلك المنزوح (طهارة للبئر، والدلو والرشاء) والبكرة ويد) المستقى) روي ذلك عن أبي يوسف والحسن، لأن نجاسة هذه الأشياء كانت بنجاسة الماء فتكون طهارتها بطهارته نفياً للحرج، كطهارة دنّ الخمر بتخللها، وطهارة عروة الإبريق بطهارة اليد إذا أخذها كلما غسل يده، وروي عن أبي يوسف أن الأربع من الفيران كفارة واحدة والخمس كالدجاجة إلى التسع والعشر كالشاة وقال محمد الثلاث إلى الخمس كالهرة والست كالكلب وهو ظاهر الرواية، وما كان بين الفأرة والهرة فحكمه حكم الفأرة، وما كان بين الهرة والكلب فحكمه حكم الهرة، وإن وقع فأرة وهرة فهما كهرة ويدخل الأقل في الأكثر قوله : (إلى خمسين) هو المذكور في الجامع الصغير قال في الهداية : وهو الأظهر اهـ لأن الجامع الصغير آخر التصنيفين فالمذكور فيه هو المرجوع إليه. قوله : (أو) ستين) هي رواية الأصل قال في شرح المجمع : وهو الأحوط قوله: (بعد إخراجه) راجع إلى الواقع من حيث هو لأن النزح قبله لا يفيد لأنه سبب النجاسة إلا إذا تعذر إخراجه كخشبة، أو خرقة نجسة تعذر إخراجها أو تغيبت فينزح القدر الواجب، وتطهر الخشبة والخرقة تبعاً لطهارة البئر كما في السراج قوله : (لاحتمال زيادة الخ) روى الأكمل الحديث المذكور بلفظ في الفأرة إذا وقعت في البئر ينزح عشرون دلواً، أو ثلاثون رواه السمرقندي بالشك، وأولاً حد الشيئين فكان الأقل، وهو العشرون ثابتاً بيقين، وثبت الشك في الأكثر فكان مستحباً لئلا يترك اللفظ المروي اهـ.
Book of Purification. The ruling for a chicken and what is similar to it is that it gives its ruling, and it is recommended to increase to fifty or sixty due to what was related from 'Ata' and al-Sha'bi. If a mouse dies in it (the well) - with the hamzah - or something similar like a sparrow, and it has not bloated, then twenty buckets must be removed after taking it out, due to the saying of Anas (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding a mouse that died in the well and was taken out immediately: 'Twenty buckets are removed.' And it is recommended to increase to thirty due to the possibility that the bucket mentioned in the tradition may be larger than what was estimated as the medium size. And that removed water was a purification for the well, the bucket, the rope, the pulley, and the hand of the one drawing water. This was related from Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan, because the impurity of these things was due to the impurity of the water, so their purification is through its purification to remove hardship, like the purification of a wine jar by its transformation into vinegar, and the purification of the handle of a jug by the purification of the hand when taking it every time one washes one's hand. And it is related from Abu Yusuf that four mice count as one atonement, five like a chicken up to nine, and ten like a sheep. And Muhammad said: three to five like a cat, six like a dog, and that is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). Whatever is between a mouse and a cat has the ruling of the mouse, and whatever is between a cat and a dog has the ruling of the cat. If a mouse and a cat fall in, they count as a cat, and the lesser is included in the greater. His saying: '(to fifty)' is what is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. He said in al-Hidayah: 'It is the more apparent.' End. Because al-Jami' al-Saghir is the later of the two compositions, so what is mentioned in it is what is referred back to. His saying: '(or sixty)' is the narration of the original text. He said in Sharh al-Majma': 'It is more precautionary.' His saying: '(after removing it)' refers to the occurrence in itself, because removal before that is not beneficial, as it is the cause of impurity, unless its removal is not possible, like a piece of wood or a soiled rag whose removal is not possible or it has been lost, then the obligatory amount is removed, and the wood and rag become pure following the purification of the well, as in al-Siraj. His saying: '(due to the possibility of increase, etc.)' Al-Akmal related the mentioned hadith with the wording: 'If a mouse falls into the well, twenty buckets are removed, or thirty.' Al-Samarqandi related it with doubt. He resolved the two possibilities by taking the lesser, so twenty is established with certainty, and doubt arises concerning the greater, so it is recommended, so as not to abandon the transmitted wording. End.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage from the matn and hashiya establishes the ruling for when a mouse (or similar small animal like a sparrow) dies in a well without bloating. The obligatory (wajib) action is to remove twenty buckets of water after extracting the animal. This is based on the hadith of Anas ibn Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) which is cited as the proof. Additionally, it is recommended (mustahabb) to increase to thirty buckets due to a variant narration with doubt. The hashiya clarifies that the ruling for a chicken and similar animals is like that of a mouse, but the recommended amount is higher (fifty or sixty). The purification of the well also purifies the bucket, rope, pulley, and the hand of the person drawing water, by analogy to a wine jar becoming pure when the wine turns to vinegar, or a jug handle becoming pure when the hand is washed. The passage includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf specifies different amounts for multiple mice, while Muhammad al-Shaybani (whose view is the apparent narration) grades animals by size: mouse, cat, dog, etc. The ruling balances the texts and removes hardship.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): Removal of water from a well to purge it of impurity
فأرة (fa'rah): A mouse or rat; the standard case for small animals falling into a well
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted texts of Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) in the six core books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the obligatory amount of water to remove from a well when a mouse dies in it without bloating?
A) Ten buckets
B) Twenty buckets
C) Thirty buckets
D) Fifty buckets

Key Takeaway
The obligatory removal of twenty buckets for a mouse in a well is based on a hadith, and the recommended increase to thirty handles textual doubt; the Hanafi school resolves intra-madhhab differences by relying on the apparent narration of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Purity of Droppings of Edible Birds (Pigeon and Sparrow) – Istihsan and Evidence
Ruling on droppings of edible birds with istihsan evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on droppings of edible birds (pigeon, sparrow) and explain the proof of istihsan based on the hadith of the pigeon and practice of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
Pigeon and sparrow droppings are pure (tahir) by istihsan, not impure like other birds.

One of the common questions in ritual purity is whether bird droppings are impure (najis). The Hanafi school makes a clear distinction: droppings of edible birds such as pigeons and sparrows are considered pure, based on juristic preference (istihsan) rooted in the Prophet's treatment of pigeons and the practice of the Sahaba. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the exceptions of chicken and goose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
The text asks: do droppings of pigeons and sparrows spoil well water? The general principle is that if a substance is najis, it spoils the water. So the student must first determine the ruling on the substance itself.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling
The matn states: 'And it does not spoil water with the droppings of a pigeon, nor with the droppings of a sparrow and the like of what is eaten from birds except chicken and goose.' So the ruling is that these droppings do not spoil water; they are considered pure (tahir).
3
Analyze the proof: istihsan
The text explicitly says the ruling is by istihsan. The evidence is the hadith that the Prophet (ﷺ) praised the pigeon that nested at the mouth of the cave, and the practice of Ibn Mas'ud wiping pigeon droppings with his finger. Istihsan overrides the general rule that animal waste is najis.
The hadith is cited as: 'لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم شكر الحمامة وقال: إنها أوكرت على باب الغار حتى سلمت فجازاها الله تعالى المسجد مأواها.'
4
Understand the exception: chicken and goose
The text exempts chicken and goose from this ruling. Why? Because these birds are often fed with impure substances and their droppings are considered najis by analogy. The student should note that duck is similar (included in the exception).
5
Apply the ruling to other edible birds
The phrase 'and the like' indicates that any bird whose meat is eaten (other than chicken, goose, duck) follows the same ruling: its droppings are pure. This includes, for example, quail, partridge, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع في الخانية جلد الآدمي، أو لحمه إذا وقع في الماء إن كان مقدار الظفر يفسده وإن كان دونه ولا يفسده، ولو سقط الظفر نفسه في الماء لا يفسد وفيها بول الهرة، والفأرة وخرؤهما نجس في أظهر الروايات يفسد الماء، والثوب، وبول الخفاش وخرؤه لا يفسد لتعذر الاحتراز عنه انتهى وفي الشرنبلالية عن الفيض الأصح أن البئر لا تنجس ببول الفأرة. قوله : (في ظاهر الرواية الأولى أن يقول في الصحيح فإن ظاهر الرواية كما ذكره  كتاب الطهارة ۳۹ الصحيح، ولا فرق بين الرطب واليابس والصحيح المنكسر في ظاهر الرواية لشمول الضرورة، فلا تنجس إلا أن يكون كثيراً وهو ما يستكثره الناظر) والقليل ما يستقله، وعليه الاعتماد (أو أن لا يخلو دلو عن بعرة) ونحوها كما صححه في المبسوط (ولا يفسد) أي لا ينجس (الماء بخرء حمام الخرء بالفتح واحد الخرء بالضم مثل قرء وقرء وعن الجوهري بالضم كجند وجنود والواو بعد الراء غلط (و) لا ينجس بخرء (عصفور) ونحوها مما يؤكل من الطيور غير الدجاج والأوز والحكم بطهارته استحسان لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم شكر الحمامة وقال : إنها أوكرت على باب الغار حتى سلمت فجازاها الله تعالى المسجد مأواها، فهو دليل على طهارة ما يكون منها، ومسح ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه خرء الحمامة عنه بأصبعه، والاختيار في كثير من كتب المذهب طهارته عندنا واختلف التصحيح في طهارة خرء ما لا يؤكل من الطيور ونجاسته مخففاً (ولا) ينجس الماء ولا المائعات قوله : الخرء) بالفتح الخ في القاموس والخرء بالضم العذرة ج خروء وخرآن وكذا في الصحاح، كما ذكره، وقوله والواو بعد الراء الخ أي كتابتها بعد الراء في المفرد على الأصح (بموت أن السرخسي أن الروث، والمتفتت من البعر مفسد مطلقاً قوله : (ونحوها) الأولى التذكير إلا يعود على المذكور كله. قوله: (غير الدجاج والاوز) مثلهما البط قوله : (لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الخ) ولأن الصدر الأول، ومن بعدهم أجمعوا على اقتناء الحمامات في المساجد حتى في المسجد الحرام مع الأمر بتطهيرها فدل ظاهراً على عدم نجاسته. قوله: (ومسح ابن مسعود) وكذلك ابن عمر إلا أنه مسحه بحصاة قوله : واختلف التصحيح الخ) قال في الخانية : وزرق ما لا يؤكل لحمه من الطيور لا يفسد الماء في ظاهر الرواية عند محمد لتعذر الاحتراز عنه، ثم قال بعد ذلك : وزرق سباع الطيور يفسد الثوب إذا فحش، ويفسد ماء الأواني ولا يفسد ماء البئر اهـ.
تنبيه : قال في النهاية : الاستحالة إلى فساد لا توجب نجاسة فإن سائر الأطعمة تفسد بطول المكث، ولا تنجس اهـ لكن يحرم الأكل في هذه الحالة للإيذاء لا للنجاسة، كاللحم إذا أنتن يحرم أكله، ولا يصير نجساً بخلاف السمن واللبن والدهن، والزيت إذا أنتن لا يحرم، وكذا الأشربة لا تحرم بالتغير كذا في البحر، ويتفرع على حرمة أكل اللحم إذا أنتن للإيذاء للنجاسة حرمة أكل الفسيخ المعروف في الديار المصرية لما ذكر، ولم أره صريحاً وفي تذكرة الحكيم داود عند ذكره السمك قال : والمقدّد الشهير بالفسيخ رديء يولد السدد، والقولنج والحصا والبلغم الحصى، وربما أوقع في الحميات الربعية، والسل ويهزل اهـ. قوله : (على الأصح) الخلاف في غير السمك، أما هو فلا يفسد المائع إجماعاً.
Branches from al-Khaniyya: If human skin or flesh falls into water, if it is the size of a fingernail it spoils it, otherwise not. If a fingernail itself falls into water, it does not spoil it. In it: the urine of a cat and a mouse and their droppings are najis in the most apparent narrations, spoiling water and clothing. The urine and droppings of a bat do not spoil due to the impossibility of avoidance. End. In al-Sharnablaliyya from al-Fayd: the soundest view is that the well is not impurified by mouse urine. His saying: 'in the apparent narration the first, meaning in the sound, then the apparent narration as he mentioned.' [PAGE_039] Book of Purification 39: 'the sound, and there is no difference between wet and dry and the sound and broken in the apparent narration due to the inclusion of necessity, so it is not impurified unless it is a lot, which is what the observer considers much, and the little is what he considers little, and upon it is reliance, or that the bucket is not free of a dung pellet and the like' as corrected in al-Mabsut. 'And it does not spoil' i.e. does not impurify 'water with the droppings of a pigeon' - al-khar' with fatha is the singular of khur' with damma like qur' and qur', and from al-Jawhari with damma like jund and junud, and the waw after the ra is a mistake. 'And it does not impurify with the droppings of a sparrow and the like of what is eaten from birds except chicken and goose.' The ruling of its purity is by istihsan, because the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) thanked the pigeon and said: 'It nested at the mouth of the cave until he was saved, so Allah rewarded it with the mosque as its abode.' So it is evidence for the purity of what comes from it. And Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) wiped the pigeon's droppings from himself with his finger. The choice in many books of the school is its purity according to us. The correct opinion differs regarding the purity of the droppings of what is not eaten from birds and its light impurity. 'And it does not impurify water nor liquids.' His saying: 'al-khar' with fatha, etc.' In al-Qamus: al-khur' with damma is feces, plurals khuru' and khur'an. Similarly in al-Sihah. And his saying: 'and the waw after the ra' means its writing after the ra in the singular is correct. 'It dies...' that al-Sarakhsi said: dung and crumbled dung pellets are absolutely spoiling. His saying: 'and the like' - the masculine is preferred unless it refers to the whole mentioned. His saying: 'except chicken and goose' - duck is similar. His saying: 'because the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace), etc.' And because the early generations and those after them unanimously agreed on keeping pigeons in the mosques, even in the Sacred Mosque, despite the order to purify them, so it apparently indicates their non-impurity. His saying: 'and Ibn Mas'ud wiped' - similarly Ibn 'Umar, except he wiped it with a pebble. His saying: 'the correct opinion differs, etc.' He said in al-Khaniyya: The droppings of what is not eaten from birds do not spoil water in the apparent narration according to Muhammad due to the impossibility of avoidance. Then he said afterwards: The droppings of birds of prey spoil clothing if it is copious, and spoil water of vessels but do not spoil well water. End. Warning: He said in al-Nihaya: Transformation into corruption does not necessitate impurity, for all foods spoil by long duration and are not impure. End. But eating is forbidden in this case due to harm, not impurity, like meat that becomes putrid - it is forbidden to eat but does not become impure. Contrary to samn, milk, oil, and olive oil when they become putrid - they are not forbidden. Likewise, beverages are not forbidden by change, as in al-Bahr. And it branches from the prohibition of eating meat when it becomes putrid due to harm, not impurity, the prohibition of eating the fisikh (fermented fish) known in the Egyptian lands due to what was mentioned, though I have not seen it explicitly. In the Tadhkira of al-Hakim Dawud when mentioning fish, he said: 'The dried known as fisikh is harmful, causing blockages, colic, gravel, phlegm gravel, and sometimes causes quartan fevers, consumption, and emaciation.' End. His saying: 'on the soundest' - the dispute is concerning other than fish; as for fish, it does not spoil the liquid by consensus.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This part continues with furu' (branches) from al-Khaniyya concerning human skin and nails falling into water, and the urine and droppings of cats, mice, and bats. The key principle is that if avoidance is impossible (like bat droppings), they do not spoil water. Then the hashiya explains the matn's ruling on droppings of camels, cows, and horses: they do not spoil well water whether the droppings are wet or dry, unless they are abundant as judged by the observer. For bird droppings, the matn states that pigeon and sparrow droppings (from birds whose meat is eaten, except chicken and goose) do not spoil water. The proof is istihsan based on the Prophet's praise for the pigeon at the cave and Ibn Mas'ud's wiping of pigeon droppings with his finger. The early Muslims kept pigeons in mosques, implying purity. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Muhammad holds that droppings of non-edible birds do not spoil water due to necessity, but birds of prey spoil clothing and vessel water. A final note clarifies that mere spoilage (istihalah to corruption) does not cause impurity; e.g., putrid meat is forbidden to eat due to harm but remains pure. However, foods like milk and oil do not become forbidden when rancid. The ruling on fisikh (fermented fish) is discussed, but the author did not find it explicit.

Key Terms
خرء (khar'): Droppings or excrement, specifically of birds
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference based on necessity and public ease

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi evidence for considering droppings of edible birds (like sparrows) pure?
A) Quranic verse on purification
B) Hadith of the Prophet praising the pigeon at the cave and Ibn Mas'ud's action
C) Qiyas on cow dung
D) Consensus of all scholars

Key Takeaway
Pigeon and sparrow droppings are pure (tahir) by istihsan, based on prophetic practice and early Muslim consensus, making them an exception to the general impurity of animal waste.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Non-Edible Bird Droppings and the Principle of Istihalah to Corruption
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on non-edible bird droppings and principle of istihalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on non-edible bird droppings, distinguish between Muhammad's view and others, and apply the principle that istihalah to corruption does not cause impurity.

Core Idea
Droppings of non-edible birds do not spoil water in wells, but birds of prey spoil clothing; mere spoilage does not make a substance najis.

After establishing the purity of edible bird droppings, the text turns to non-edible birds. Here, there is an important intra-Hanafi difference of opinion. Additionally, the passage introduces a crucial principle: when a substance undergoes istihalah (transformation) to corruption, it does not become impure. This has practical implications for food items like putrid meat and fermented fish (fisikh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject: non-edible birds
The text refers to 'what is not eaten from birds' and 'birds of prey'. Determine which birds fall into this category (e.g., eagles, hawks, crows).
2
Understand Muhammad's position
According to Imam Muhammad, droppings of non-edible birds do not spoil water in wells, even if copious, due to the impossibility of avoidance. This is in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah).
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf
The text says 'the correct opinion differs regarding the purity of droppings of what is not eaten and its light impurity'. Some Hanafis hold it to be lightly impure (najis mukhaffaf). The commentator refers to al-Khaniyya, which distinguishes between well water and vessel water: birds of prey spoil vessel water but not well water.
4
Extract the principle on istihalah
The passage includes a warning (tanbih) that transformation (istihalah) to corruption (like putrefaction) does not cause impurity. Foods that spoil over time remain pure, though eating them may be prohibited if harmful.
Example: meat that becomes putrid is forbidden to eat due to harm (adha), not because it becomes impure.
5
Apply the principle to fisikh (fermented fish)
The commentator applies this to fisikh: it is not impure by istihalah, but its consumption may be prohibited due to harm. The author notes he has not seen an explicit ruling, but cites medical sources on its harm.
This shows the process of deriving fatwa based on principles.

تنبيه : قال في النهاية : الاستحالة إلى فساد لا توجب نجاسة فإن سائر الأطعمة تفسد بطول المكث، ولا تنجس اهـ لكن يحرم الأكل في هذه الحالة للإيذاء لا للنجاسة، كاللحم إذا أنتن يحرم أكله، ولا يصير نجساً بخلاف السمن واللبن والدهن، والزيت إذا أنتن لا يحرم، وكذا الأشربة لا تحرم بالتغير كذا في البحر، ويتفرع على حرمة أكل اللحم إذا أنتن للإيذاء للنجاسة حرمة أكل الفسيخ المعروف في الديار المصرية لما ذكر، ولم أره صريحاً وفي تذكرة الحكيم داود عند ذكره السمك قال : والمقدّد الشهير بالفسيخ رديء يولد السدد، والقولنج والحصا والبلغم الحصى، وربما أوقع في الحميات الربعية، والسل ويهزل اهـ. قوله : (على الأصح) الخلاف في غير السمك، أما هو فلا يفسد المائع إجماعاً.
Branches from al-Khaniyya: If human skin or flesh falls into water, if it is the size of a fingernail it spoils it, otherwise not. If a fingernail itself falls into water, it does not spoil it. In it: the urine of a cat and a mouse and their droppings are najis in the most apparent narrations, spoiling water and clothing. The urine and droppings of a bat do not spoil due to the impossibility of avoidance. End. In al-Sharnablaliyya from al-Fayd: the soundest view is that the well is not impurified by mouse urine. His saying: 'in the apparent narration the first, meaning in the sound, then the apparent narration as he mentioned.' [PAGE_039] Book of Purification 39: 'the sound, and there is no difference between wet and dry and the sound and broken in the apparent narration due to the inclusion of necessity, so it is not impurified unless it is a lot, which is what the observer considers much, and the little is what he considers little, and upon it is reliance, or that the bucket is not free of a dung pellet and the like' as corrected in al-Mabsut. 'And it does not spoil' i.e. does not impurify 'water with the droppings of a pigeon' - al-khar' with fatha is the singular of khur' with damma like qur' and qur', and from al-Jawhari with damma like jund and junud, and the waw after the ra is a mistake. 'And it does not impurify with the droppings of a sparrow and the like of what is eaten from birds except chicken and goose.' The ruling of its purity is by istihsan, because the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) thanked the pigeon and said: 'It nested at the mouth of the cave until he was saved, so Allah rewarded it with the mosque as its abode.' So it is evidence for the purity of what comes from it. And Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) wiped the pigeon's droppings from himself with his finger. The choice in many books of the school is its purity according to us. The correct opinion differs regarding the purity of the droppings of what is not eaten from birds and its light impurity. 'And it does not impurify water nor liquids.' His saying: 'al-khar' with fatha, etc.' In al-Qamus: al-khur' with damma is feces, plurals khuru' and khur'an. Similarly in al-Sihah. And his saying: 'and the waw after the ra' means its writing after the ra in the singular is correct. 'It dies...' that al-Sarakhsi said: dung and crumbled dung pellets are absolutely spoiling. His saying: 'and the like' - the masculine is preferred unless it refers to the whole mentioned. His saying: 'except chicken and goose' - duck is similar. His saying: 'because the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace), etc.' And because the early generations and those after them unanimously agreed on keeping pigeons in the mosques, even in the Sacred Mosque, despite the order to purify them, so it apparently indicates their non-impurity. His saying: 'and Ibn Mas'ud wiped' - similarly Ibn 'Umar, except he wiped it with a pebble. His saying: 'the correct opinion differs, etc.' He said in al-Khaniyya: The droppings of what is not eaten from birds do not spoil water in the apparent narration according to Muhammad due to the impossibility of avoidance. Then he said afterwards: The droppings of birds of prey spoil clothing if it is copious, and spoil water of vessels but do not spoil well water. End. Warning: He said in al-Nihaya: Transformation into corruption does not necessitate impurity, for all foods spoil by long duration and are not impure. End. But eating is forbidden in this case due to harm, not impurity, like meat that becomes putrid - it is forbidden to eat but does not become impure. Contrary to samn, milk, oil, and olive oil when they become putrid - they are not forbidden. Likewise, beverages are not forbidden by change, as in al-Bahr. And it branches from the prohibition of eating meat when it becomes putrid due to harm, not impurity, the prohibition of eating the fisikh (fermented fish) known in the Egyptian lands due to what was mentioned, though I have not seen it explicitly. In the Tadhkira of al-Hakim Dawud when mentioning fish, he said: 'The dried known as fisikh is harmful, causing blockages, colic, gravel, phlegm gravel, and sometimes causes quartan fevers, consumption, and emaciation.' End. His saying: 'on the soundest' - the dispute is concerning other than fish; as for fish, it does not spoil the liquid by consensus.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This part continues with furu' (branches) from al-Khaniyya concerning human skin and nails falling into water, and the urine and droppings of cats, mice, and bats. The key principle is that if avoidance is impossible (like bat droppings), they do not spoil water. Then the hashiya explains the matn's ruling on droppings of camels, cows, and horses: they do not spoil well water whether the droppings are wet or dry, unless they are abundant as judged by the observer. For bird droppings, the matn states that pigeon and sparrow droppings (from birds whose meat is eaten, except chicken and goose) do not spoil water. The proof is istihsan based on the Prophet's praise for the pigeon at the cave and Ibn Mas'ud's wiping of pigeon droppings with his finger. The early Muslims kept pigeons in mosques, implying purity. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Muhammad holds that droppings of non-edible birds do not spoil water due to necessity, but birds of prey spoil clothing and vessel water. A final note clarifies that mere spoilage (istihalah to corruption) does not cause impurity; e.g., putrid meat is forbidden to eat due to harm but remains pure. However, foods like milk and oil do not become forbidden when rancid. The ruling on fisikh (fermented fish) is discussed, but the author did not find it explicit.

Key Terms
استحالة (istihalah): Transformation of a substance into another state, e.g., putrefaction
فسيخ (fisikh): Fermented or dried fish, a common food in Egypt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to a well if a crow's dropping falls into it?
A) The well becomes impure
B) The well becomes impure only if the dropping is abundant
C) The well remains pure because crow droppings are pure in wells by Muhammad's view
D) The well becomes impure but can be purified with a bucket of water

Key Takeaway
Non-edible bird droppings do not spoil well water per the mufta bihi; istihalah to corruption does not cause impurity, but may render food haram if harmful.

Ruling on Bird Droppings in Water: Purity Based on Istihsan and Evidence
Explains ruling with evidence and internal ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bird droppings in water, explain the evidence, and identify the intra-madhhab difference regarding non-edible birds.

Core Idea
Pigeon droppings are pure by istihsan, not strict qiyas, based on prophetic practice and companion action.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on water impurity from bird droppings. The text from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains that droppings of edible birds (pigeon, sparrow) do not render water impure, relying on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than analogy. The evidence includes a prophetic tradition about the pigeon's role in the Hijra and the practice of Ibn Mas'ud. We also explore the internal Hanafi difference regarding non-edible birds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what falls into water (bird droppings) and which bird (edible vs non-edible). The ruling: water is not impure for edible birds.
Edible birds include pigeon, sparrow; exclude chicken, goose, duck.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith: the Prophet thanked the pigeon for nesting at the cave and made the mosque its abode. Also, Ibn Mas'ud wiped droppings with his finger.
These are considered evidence of purity.
3
Determine the method of reasoning
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because strict qiyas would rule impurity, but istihsan considers necessity and ease.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in Hanafi school.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For non-edible birds, there is difference in tashih: some hold impurity with takhfif, while the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) at Muhammad holds they do not spoil water.
The word 'ikhtalafa al-tashih' indicates divergence on which view is sahih.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
For edible birds, purity is the relied-upon position. For non-edible, the preferred view (from al-Khaniyah) is that they do not spoil water.
Authority: zahir al-riwayah and later fatwa works.

ولا ينجس الماء بخرء حمام الخرء بالفتح واحد الخرء بالضم مثل قرء وقرء وعن الجوهري بالضم كجند وجنود والواو بعد الراء غلط (و) لا ينجس بخرء عصفور ونحوها مما يؤكل من الطيور غير الدجاج والأوز والحكم بطهارته استحسان لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم شكر الحمامة وقال : إنها أوكرت على باب الغار حتى سلمت فجازاها الله تعالى المسجد مأواها، فهو دليل على طهارة ما يكون منها، ومسح ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه خرء الحمامة عنه بأصبعه، والاختيار في كثير من كتب المذهب طهارته عندنا واختلف التصحيح في طهارة خرء ما لا يؤكل من الطيور ونجاسته مخففاً قوله : (الخرء) بالفتح الخ في القاموس والخرء بالضم العذرة ج خروء وخرآن وكذا في الصحاح، كما ذكره، وقوله والواو بعد الراء الخ أي كتابتها بعد الراء في المفرد على الأصح (بموت أن السرخسي أن الروث، والمتفتت من البعر مفسد مطلقاً قوله : (ونحوها) الأولى التذكير إلا يعود على المذكور كله. قوله: (غير الدجاج والاوز) مثلهما البط قوله : (لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الخ) ولأن الصدر الأول، ومن بعدهم أجمعوا على اقتناء الحمامات في المساجد حتى في المسجد الحرام مع الأمر بتطهيرها فدل ظاهراً على عدم نجاسته. قوله: (ومسح ابن مسعود) وكذلك ابن عمر إلا أنه مسحه بحصاة قوله : واختلف التصحيح الخ) قال في الخانية : وزرق ما لا يؤكل لحمه من الطيور لا يفسد الماء في ظاهر الرواية عند محمد لتعذر الاحتراز عنه، ثم قال بعد ذلك : وزرق سباع الطيور يفسد الثوب إذا فحش، ويفسد ماء الأواني ولا يفسد ماء البئر اهـ.
And water is not rendered impure by the droppings of a pigeon – droppings (al-khar') with a fatha, singular of khar' with a damma, like qar' and qur'; and from al-Jawhari: with damma like junud and junud, and the waw after the ra is a mistake. And it is not rendered impure by the droppings of a sparrow and similar [birds] from what is eaten of birds, except for chickens and geese. The ruling of its purity is by istihsan because the Prophet ﷺ thanked the pigeon and said: 'It nested at the mouth of the cave until [they] were safe, so Allah rewarded it by making the mosque its abode.' This is evidence for the purity of what comes from it. And Ibn Mas'ud رضي الله عنه wiped pigeon droppings off himself with his finger. The preferred view in many books of the madhhab is its purity with us, and the sahih [view] differs regarding the purity of droppings of what is not eaten of birds and its impurity with takhfif. His saying: (al-khar') with a fatha etc. in al-Qamus, and al-khar' with a damma is filth, plural khuru' and khar'an, and likewise in al-Sihah as he mentioned. And his saying: and the waw after the ra etc., meaning its writing after the ra in the singular is on the most correct [view]. (By the death of) that al-Sarakhsi: that dung and crumbled pellets spoil absolutely. His saying: (and similar) the preferred is masculine reference unless it refers to all that was mentioned. His saying: (except chickens and geese) similarly ducks. His saying: (because the Prophet ﷺ etc.) and because the early generations and those after them agreed on keeping pigeons in mosques, even in al-Masjid al-Haram, with the command to purify them, so this outwardly indicates their non-najasa. His saying: (and Ibn Mas'ud wiped) and likewise Ibn 'Umar, except that he wiped it with a pebble. His saying: (and the sahih differs etc.) He said in al-Khaniyah: The droppings of birds whose meat is not eaten do not spoil water in the apparent narration according to Muhammad, due to difficulty of guarding against it. Then he said after that: The droppings of birds of prey spoil the garment if excessive, and spoil water in vessels, but do not spoil well water.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of Maraqi al-Falah, dealing with the ruling on bird droppings falling into water. The author begins by affirming the Hanafi ruling that droppings of pigeons and sparrows (and similar edible birds, excluding chickens and geese) do not render water impure. This ruling is based on istihsan, not qiyas, due to the difficulty of avoiding such droppings. The evidence includes a hadith that the Prophet ﷺ praised a pigeon for nesting at the mouth of the cave during the Hijra, and that Allah granted pigeons the mosque as a dwelling, implying their droppings are pure. Additionally, Ibn Mas'ud (and Ibn 'Umar) wiped off pigeon droppings with his finger or a pebble, indicating they did not consider them impure. The preferred view in the Hanafi school is purity for edible birds. However, there is a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf fi al-tashih) regarding the droppings of non-edible birds. The commentator then clarifies lexical points: the word 'khar' (droppings) and the spelling of waw after ra. The commentary from al-Khaniyah states that according to Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the droppings of non-edible birds do not spoil water in the apparent narration of the school (zahir al-riwayah), due to difficulty of avoidance. However, droppings of birds of prey spoil garments if excessive and spoil water in vessels, but not well water. This shows a distinction within the Hanafi school: edible birds' droppings are pure, non-edible birds' droppings are considered not spoiling in the zahir al-riwayah, but birds of prey have a different ruling regarding garments and vessels.

Key Terms
خرء (khar'): Bird droppings or dung, specifically of birds. In this context, it is used for pigeon and sparrow droppings.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas. Here, the purity of bird droppings is based on istihsan due to necessity and ease.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The manifest or well-known transmission of the Hanafi school, from the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the evidence cited for the purity of pigeon droppings?
A) Qiyas to horse urine
B) Hadith of the Prophet thanking the pigeon and Ibn Mas'ud's action
C) Consensus of all madhahib
D) Text from the Quran

Key Takeaway
Edible bird droppings are pure by istihsan, supported by prophetic and companion practice; non-edible bird droppings are governed by a nuanced ikhtilaf with the preferred view of not spoiling water.

Spoilage Does Not Cause Impurity: The Principle of Istihalah
Explains that spoilage does not cause impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that transformation into spoilage does not cause impurity and distinguish between prohibition due to harm versus impurity.

Core Idea
Spoiled food is haram due to harm, not impurity – the two are legally distinct.

This passage clarifies a critical distinction: transformation (istihalah) into spoilage does not render an object impure (najis). Foods may become prohibited to eat due to harm (adha), but their substance remains pure. This principle governs rulings on putrid meat, rancid oil, and even fermented fish. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between damage and impurity in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the General Ruling
Locate the initial statement: 'Transformation into spoilage does not necessitate impurity.' This is a general principle: spoilage (istihalah ila fasad) does not make something najis.
2
Distinguish Harm from Impurity
The text then says: 'but eating them in this state is prohibited due to harm, not impurity.' Here, 'harm' (adha) – e.g., putrid meat – causes prohibition, but does not change the substance's purity. Understand that impurity and harm are separate legal categories.
3
Apply the Ruling to Specific Examples
Work through the examples: putrid meat (haram but pure), ghee and oil (not haram when putrid), and drinks (not haram). Then the extension to salted fish (fseekh) – its prohibition is due to proven harm, not impurity. Note the medical evidence cited.
This shows how the principle is applied consistently across different food items.

His saying: (and it protects itself with the wing that has the disease) – some virtuous people said: I examined that wing and found it to be the left. Branch: A fresh egg that falls from a chicken's belly does not make the liquid impure, nor does a fetus that falls from its mother's belly, even if moist, as long as one does not know that there is any [impurity] on them, because the moisture of the exit is not impure. Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the commentary on Maraqi al-Falah, covering multiple related rulings. It begins with a general principle: transformation (istihalah) into spoilage does not cause impurity. Foods that spoil over time become prohibited due to harm (adha) but not due to impurity (najasa). Meat that becomes putrid is haram to eat but remains pure, unlike ghee, oil, etc., which do not become haram when putrid. The commentator then extends this to salted fish (fseekh), noting its harm based on medical sources. Next, the text addresses the ruling on dead animals without blood. The key rule: any animal that has no flowing blood (la dam lahu sayil), whether land or sea, does not render water or other liquids impure when it dies in them. Examples include fish (samak), frogs (dafda'), water animals like crabs, water dogs, and water pigs. For land creatures, the frog is treated differently: if it has flowing blood, it spoils the water; if not, it does not. The text then lists various insects and small animals whose death does not spoil water: bedbugs, flies, hornets, scorpions, beetles, locusts, fleas, and lice. The primary evidence is the hadith of the fly: the Prophet commanded that if a fly falls into a drink, one should immerse it completely then remove it, because one wing has disease and the other has cure. The commentator explains the deduction: if the fly's death made the liquid impure, the Prophet would not have commanded immersion, as that would kill the fly and cause impurity; rather, he would have forbidden it. Therefore, the fly's death does not make the liquid impure. This is a strong dalil naqli (textual proof). The passage also discusses the definition of 'no blood' – it means no flowing blood; if blood is present but not flowing, the animal is still considered without blood. For aquatic animals, a distinction is made: those that live only in water (like fish) do not spoil the water; those that live both on land and water (like ducks, geese) – there is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: Qadikhan says they spoil, while al-Mujtaba says they do not, and the first view is more likely. However, the water remains pure but its consumption is makruh due to disgust. The passage ends with a branch ruling: a fresh egg from a chicken or a fetus (sakhla) that falls into a liquid does not make it impure, even if moist, unless one knows there is impurity on them, because the moisture from the birth canal is not impure.

Key Terms
استحالة إلى فساد (istihalah ila fasad): Transformation into spoilage or putrefaction. This does not cause impurity (najasa) but may affect permissibility due to harm.
فسيخ (fseekh): A type of salted, fermented fish commonly prepared in Egypt. The Hanafi ruling is that it is haram due to harm, not impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on ghee that has become rancid (putrid)?
A) It becomes impure and must be discarded.
B) It remains pure but is prohibited to eat.
C) It remains pure and is permissible to eat.
D) It becomes impure but may be used for non-food purposes.

Key Takeaway
Spoilage (istihalah ila fasad) does not cause impurity; only harm (adha) can prohibit consumption, and this varies by substance.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Animals Without Blood Do Not Spoil Water: The Fly Hadith
Explains the ruling on dead animals without blood in water, with proof from the fly hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which creatures are considered 'without flowing blood' and explain how the hadith of the fly proves that their death does not spoil liquids.

Core Idea
A fly's death does not spoil a drink – the Prophet's command to immerse it proves purity.

The Hanafi school rules that any animal without flowing blood – including fish, frogs, flies, and scorpions – does not make water or other liquids impure when it dies in them. This is supported by the well-known hadith of the fly, where the Prophet instructed to immerse a fly that falls into a drink. The deduction is clear: if its death caused impurity, he would not have ordered immersion that kills it. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof, and the categories of animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The text states the core rule: animals without flowing blood (la dam lahu) do not spoil water or other liquids when they die in them. This includes both land and sea creatures like fish, flies, scorpions, etc.
The key condition is the absence of flowing blood (sayil), not the absolute absence of blood.
2
List the Categories and Exceptions
Work through the list: fish (samak), frogs (dafda'), water animals (crab, water dog, water pig), insects (baqq, dhubab, zanbur, 'aqrab, etc.). Note the exception: land frogs with flowing blood do spoil water. Also, amphibians living in both land and water have a difference of opinion.
3
Locate the Dalil and Understand Wajh al-Dalalah
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'When a fly falls into the drink of one of you, let him immerse it then remove it, for in one of its wings is disease and in the other is cure.' The command to immerse implies that the fly's presence does not make the drink impure. If it did, the Prophet would have forbidden immersion, not commanded it.
The proof uses mafhum al-mukhalafah? Actually, it's a direct indication that the drink remains pure.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Amphibians
For animals that live both on land and water (e.g., ducks, geese), Qadikhan says they spoil water, while al-Mujtaba says they do not. The commentator (al-Tahtawi) prefers the first view (awjah). Water remains pure, but drinking may be makruh if distasteful.
This shows how later scholars refined the ruling.

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the commentary on Maraqi al-Falah, covering multiple related rulings. It begins with a general principle: transformation (istihalah) into spoilage does not cause impurity. Foods that spoil over time become prohibited due to harm (adha) but not due to impurity (najasa). Meat that becomes putrid is haram to eat but remains pure, unlike ghee, oil, etc., which do not become haram when putrid. The commentator then extends this to salted fish (fseekh), noting its harm based on medical sources. Next, the text addresses the ruling on dead animals without blood. The key rule: any animal that has no flowing blood (la dam lahu sayil), whether land or sea, does not render water or other liquids impure when it dies in them. Examples include fish (samak), frogs (dafda'), water animals like crabs, water dogs, and water pigs. For land creatures, the frog is treated differently: if it has flowing blood, it spoils the water; if not, it does not. The text then lists various insects and small animals whose death does not spoil water: bedbugs, flies, hornets, scorpions, beetles, locusts, fleas, and lice. The primary evidence is the hadith of the fly: the Prophet commanded that if a fly falls into a drink, one should immerse it completely then remove it, because one wing has disease and the other has cure. The commentator explains the deduction: if the fly's death made the liquid impure, the Prophet would not have commanded immersion, as that would kill the fly and cause impurity; rather, he would have forbidden it. Therefore, the fly's death does not make the liquid impure. This is a strong dalil naqli (textual proof). The passage also discusses the definition of 'no blood' – it means no flowing blood; if blood is present but not flowing, the animal is still considered without blood. For aquatic animals, a distinction is made: those that live only in water (like fish) do not spoil the water; those that live both on land and water (like ducks, geese) – there is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: Qadikhan says they spoil, while al-Mujtaba says they do not, and the first view is more likely. However, the water remains pure but its consumption is makruh due to disgust. The passage ends with a branch ruling: a fresh egg from a chicken or a fetus (sakhla) that falls into a liquid does not make it impure, even if moist, unless one knows there is impurity on them, because the moisture from the birth canal is not impure.

Key Terms
لا دم له (la dam lahu): An animal that has no flowing blood (sayil). This includes fish, frogs, insects, etc. The criterion is the absence of flowing blood, not absolute absence of blood.
ذباب (dhubab): A fly, whose name is said to derive from 'dhab' (drive) and 'ab' (return), indicating it keeps returning when driven away.

Checkpoint
What is the evidence from the hadith that a fly's death does not make the drink impure?
A) The Prophet commanded to immerse the fly, which would kill it – if that caused impurity, he would not have commanded it.
B) The Prophet said the fly's wing contains a cure, so it must be pure.
C) The fly is too small to cause impurity.
D) The Prophet said the fly has no blood.

Key Takeaway
Animals without flowing blood do not spoil water when they die in it, as proven by the hadith of the fly, providing ease in daily life.

Animals Without Flowing Blood and Their Effect on Water
Lists creatures that do not spoil water when dying in it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on water when an animal without flowing blood dies in it and identify the dalil from the hadith of the fly.

Core Idea
A dead animal without flowing blood leaves water pure — the hadith of the fly proves it.

A common household concern is whether water becomes impure when a small insect falls into it and dies. The Hanafi school provides a clear criterion: what matters is whether the animal has flowing blood. This lesson explains the ruling based on a well-known hadith and clarifies the distinction between terrestrial and aquatic creatures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Determine whether the animal has flowing blood (nafs sa'ilah) or not. This is the key criterion.
For example, flies, mosquitoes, and fish do NOT have flowing blood.
2
State the ruling
If the animal without flowing blood dies in water (or another liquid), the water remains pure (tahir) and the dead animal is halal to eat.
3
Examine the dalil
The Prophet ﷺ commanded to immerse the fly completely when it falls into a drink, then remove it. This shows the drink remains pure; otherwise He would not order an action that causes the fly to die.
Hadith: 'When a fly falls into one's drink, immerse it then remove it, for in one wing is disease and in the other is cure.' (Bukhari, with addition by Abu Dawud)
4
Distinguish the exceptions
Terrestrial animals with flowing blood spoil the water. Animals that live both on land and water are disputed: the preferred view is that they spoil the water.
Qadi Khan holds they spoil; al-Mujtaba holds they do not. Al-Tahtawi prefers Qadi Khan's view.
5
Apply to a branch case
A fresh egg or newborn animal falling into liquid: if dry, it does not make the liquid impure. If wet (with birth fluids), there is an intra-Hanafi difference; the preferred view (from Abu Hanifa) is that it remains pure.
The moisture from the exit (birth canal) is not considered impure according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

In the meaning of an animal that has no blood, whether terrestrial or aquatic, (in) water or liquid, such as a frog. The terrestrial one spoils it if it has flowing blood. Aquatic animals like crab, water dog, water pig do not spoil it. Bug (large mosquitoes), fly, wasp, scorpion, beetle, locust, flea, louse, due to the saying: 'When a fly falls into one's drink, immerse it then remove it, for in one wing is disease and in the other is cure' (Bukhari, with addition by Abu Dawud). And his saying: 'O Salman, every food or drink that a creature without blood falls into...' Commentary: 'No blood' means no flowing blood; the criterion is lack of flow, not absence of blood entirely, so if it dies in a solid non-liquid, it does not make it impure. The distinction between aquatic and terrestrial: the aquatic cannot live out of water, the terrestrial cannot live out of land. There is disagreement about what lives in both; Qadi Khan says it spoils, al-Mujtaba says waterfowl like duck and goose do not spoil if they die in it, but the first is more correct. Branch: A fresh egg from a chicken or a newborn animal falling into liquid does not make it impure, even if wet, as long as it is not known that moisture from the exit is impure. Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the effect of various creatures dying in water or liquid. The key principle is that animals without flowing blood (e.g., fish, insects) do not spoil water when they die in it, based on the hadith of the fly. The commentary clarifies that 'no blood' means no flowing blood, so if such an animal dies in a solid substance (not liquid), it also does not make it impure. The distinction between terrestrial and aquatic animals is made: terrestrial animals with flowing blood spoil water. For animals that live both on land and water, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Qadi Khan holds they spoil water, while al-Mujtaba holds they do not; the preferred view is that they spoil. The branch regarding a fresh egg or newborn animal falling into liquid: if it is dry, it does not make the liquid impure because the moisture from the exit is not considered impure. However, if the egg or newborn is wet (with birth fluids), there is a difference: according to Abu Hanifa, it does not make the liquid impure (the moisture is not impure), but according to his two companions, it does (because it comes from an impure exit). Qadi Khan favored Abu Hanifa's view. The passage relies on the hadith about the fly to show that if a fly dies in a drink, the drink remains pure, since the Prophet commanded to immerse it completely (to extract the cure) and did not forbid drinking it.

Key Terms
ما لا نفس له سائلة (ma la nafsa lahu sa'ilah): Animals that do not have flowing blood; their death in water does not render the water impure.
البري (al-barri): Terrestrial animals; if they have flowing blood and die in water, they spoil it.
المائي (al-ma'i): Aquatic animals that cannot live out of water; they do not spoil water when they die in it.

C) The water becomes makruh (disliked) but not impure.
D) The water is pure only if the fish is removed immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that water remains pure when an animal without flowing blood dies in it, based on the hadith of the fly, with the exception of terrestrial animals having flowing blood.

Water Purity When a Person or Animal Falls In
States that a person or edible animal does not spoil water unless impure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water purity when a person or a pure-living animal falls into it, and identify the conditions that trigger impurity.

Core Idea
A living human or animal falling into water does not make it impure unless confirmed impurity or saliva reaches it.

This passage addresses a common concern: does a person (even in janabah) or an animal falling into water render it impure? The Hanafi school clarifies that living bodies are inherently pure, so water remains pure unless there is a confirmed impurity or the saliva of an impure animal is present. This ruling covers humans, edible animals, mules, donkeys, birds of prey, and wild animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Water is not made impure by the fall of a person or an edible animal (camel, cow, sheep) that exits alive and has no confirmed impurity on its body. The same applies to mules, donkeys, birds of prey, and wild animals.
The default is that bodies are pure while alive.
2
Note the conditions for purity to hold
Two conditions: (1) the being must be alive at the time of falling, (2) there must be no confirmed impurity (najasa mutayyina) on its body.
Visible impurities would spoil the water.
3
Recognize the exception via saliva
If the saliva (or sweat) of the fallen animal reaches the water, the water takes the ruling of the animal's mouth. If the mouth is impure, water becomes impure; if doubtful, mashkuk; if the animal is pure, water remains pure.
Sweat has the same ruling as saliva.
4
Distinguish water categories after saliva contact
If water becomes impure or mashkuk (doubtful), all water must be removed (nazh). If it becomes makruh, it is recommended to remove a few buckets (up to twenty) but the water is still valid for wudu without removal.
The twenty buckets are to settle the heart, not for purification.
5
Apply to specific cases
Humans (including junub, menstruating, nifas, disbeliever) are always pure while alive. Edible animals are pure unless they have a confirmed impurity. Non-edible animals (mules, donkeys, wild animals, birds of prey) are also pure while alive.
Bodies become impure only upon death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينجس الماء (بوقوع آدمي و) لا بوقوع (ما يؤكل لحمه كالإبل والبقر والغنم إذا خرج حياً ولم يكن على بدنه نجاسة) متيقنة ولا ينظر إلى ظاهر اشتمال أبوالها على أفخاذها (ولا) يفسد الماء (بوقوع بغل وحمار وسباع طير) كصفر وشاهين وحدأة (و) لا يفسد بوقوع (وحش) كسبع وقرد (في الصحيح) لطهارة بدنها، وقيل يجب نزح كل الماء إلحاقاً لرطوبتها بلعابها وإن وصل لعاب الواقع إلى الماء أخذ الماء (حكمه) طهارة، ونجاسة وكراهة، وقد علته في الأسار فينزح بالنجس، والمشكوك، وجوباً ويستحب في المكروه عدد من الدلاء لو طاهر، أو قيل عشرين قوله : (بوقوع آدمي)، ولو جنباً أو حائضاً، أو نفساء انقطع دمها، أو كافراً . قوله : (ولا ينظر الخ لاحتمال طهارتها بورودها ماء كثيراً قبل ذلك فهذا مع الأصل، وهو الطهارة تظافراً على عدم النزح، كذا في الفتح. قوله : (ولا يفسد الماء بوقوع بغل، وحمار) ولا يصير مشكوكاً لأن بدن هذه الحيوانات طاهر لأنها مخلوقة لنا استعمالاً وإنما تصير نجسة بالموت كذا في الدرر ، وهذا كله عند عدم وصول لعاب ما ذكر إلى الماء، وأما إذا وصل إليه فقد ذكر حكمه بعد قوله : ( وأن وصل لعاب الواقع الخ) وعرق كل شيء كلعابه، فيأخذ الماء حكمه أيضاً على المذهب، كذا في الدر المنتقى . قوله : (والمشكوك) صرح به المحققون من أهل المذهب وعلله الحلبي باشتراك المشكوك والنجس في عدم الطهورية وإن افترقا من حيث الطهارة فإذا لم تنزح ربما تظهر به، والصلاة به تجزي، فينزح كله قوله : (ويستحب في المكروه (عدد أي من غير تقدير في الأصل أي نزح عدد، وكذا يقال فيما بعد. قوله: (وقيل عشرين) عن محمد كل موضع فيه نزح لا ينزح أقل من العشرين، لأنه أقل ما جاء به الشرع من المقادير اهـ وهذا النزح لتسكين القلب لا للتطهير حتى لو توضاً منها من غير نزح جاز
Water is not made impure by the fall of a person, nor by the fall of what is eaten (like camel, cow, sheep) if it comes out alive and there is no confirmed impurity on its body. Nor does water spoil by the fall of a mule, donkey, birds of prey (like peregrine, hawk, kite), nor by the fall of wild animals (like lion, monkey) in the sound position, because their bodies are pure. It is said that all water must be removed due to their saliva. If the saliva of the fallen animal reaches the water, the water takes its ruling (pure, impure, or disliked). If it is impure or doubtful, removal is obligatory; for disliked, a number of buckets is recommended, up to twenty according to Muhammad. Commentary: 'Fall of a person' includes a junub, menstruating woman, woman in nifas whose blood has stopped, or a disbeliever. 'Nor does water spoil by fall of mule, donkey' because their bodies are pure, created for our use; they only become impure at death. All this when the saliva does not reach the water; if it does, the water takes the ruling of the animal's mouth. Sweat is like saliva. The doubtful (water) shares with impure in not being purifying, so all must be removed. The twenty buckets are to settle the heart, not for purification, so wudu from such water without removal is valid.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage states that a person (even in a state of major impurity or disbelief) falling into water does not make the water impure, because the human body is inherently pure. Similarly, edible land animals (like camel, cow, sheep) that fall alive and have no visible impurity do not spoil the water. The same applies to mules, donkeys, birds of prey, and wild animals; their bodies are pure while alive. However, if their saliva (or sweat) reaches the water, the water takes on the ruling of that saliva: if the animal's mouth is impure (e.g., it ate najasah), the water becomes impure; if doubtful, the water becomes doubtful. For impure or doubtful water, all the water must be removed (nazh). For water that is only makruh (disliked) due to saliva, a few buckets are recommended (up to twenty per Imam Muhammad) to ease the conscience, but the water is still valid for wudu if used without removal. The principle is that the bodies of these animals are pure as long as they are alive, and the water is not spoiled unless there is a confirmed impurity.

Key Terms
المشكوك (al-mashkuk): Water whose purity is uncertain; it shares with najis water in not being purifying, so it must be removed.
اللعاب (al-lu'ab): Saliva; if it reaches water, the water takes the ruling of the animal's mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The water becomes impure and must be removed.
B) The water remains pure and can be used for wudu.
C) The water becomes makruh and removal is recommended.
D) The water becomes mashkuk and all must be removed.

Key Takeaway
Water is not spoiled by the fall of a living human or animal unless there is a confirmed impurity or the animal's saliva reaches it.

Well Impurity from a Dead Animal: Presumption Based on Bloating
Ruling with critical refinement: conditions and presumption based on bloating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on well impurity from a dead animal, differentiating between bloated and non-bloated states, and determine when prayers must be repeated.

Core Idea
Bloating signals three days of impurity; safety mandates one day even without bloating.

Finding a dead animal in a well used for wudu raises urgent questions: when did it die? How many prayers must be repeated? The Hanafi school provides a practical presumption based on the animal's physical state—bloating indicates the passage of at least three days. This lesson unpacks that rule and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the presence of a dead animal in a well. The ruling is that the well becomes impure retroactively, requiring repetition of prayers performed with water from that well during a certain period.
2
Check the animal's condition: bloated or not
If the animal is not bloated, the impurity is presumed to have started one day and one night before discovery (imam's precaution). If bloated, the period is three days and nights.
Bloating includes hair loss or decomposition.
3
Note the condition of knowledge
These presumptions apply only if the time of death is unknown. If you know the exact time, the ruling applies from that moment without guesswork.
4
Understand the legal basis
Bloating is evidence of time passage (taqadum al-'ahd). The minimum for bloating is three days, analogized from funeral prayer custom: prayer on a grave is allowed up to three days.
5
Apply the consequence
All prayers (including wudu and ghusl) performed using that water during the presumed period must be repeated if the person was in a state of ritual impurity.
This is wajib (obligatory) according to the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجود حیوان ميت فيها أي البئر (ينجسها من يوم وليلة) عند الأمام احتياطاً (ومنتفخ) ينجسها من ثلاثة أيام ولياليها إن لم يعلم وقت وقوعه لأنّ الانتفاخ دليل تقادم العهد فيلزم إعادة صلوات تلك المدة إذا توضؤا منها وهم محدثون أو اغتسلوا من جنابة وإن كانوا متوضئين، قوله : (ووجود حيوان الخ) قيد بالحيوان لأن غيره من النجاسات لا يتأتى فيه التفصيل ولا الخلاف بل ينجسها من وقت الوجدان فقط، والمراد الحيوان الدموي غير المائي كما مر . قوله: (ومنتفخ) وبالأولى إذا كان متمعطاً، أو متفسخاً قوله : (إن لم يعلم وقت وقوعه) عبارة غيره موته بدل وقوعه، وهي الأولى، وقيد بعدم العلم لأنه إن علم أو ظن فلا أشكال ويعتبر الحكم من وقته بلا خلاف قوله : (لأن الانتفاخ دليل تقادم العهد وأدنى حد التقادم في الانتفاخ، ونحوه ثلاثة أيام لحصول ذلك في مثلها غالباً تری أن من دفن بغير صلاة يصلى على قبره إلا ثلاثة لا ،بعدها وعدم الانتفاخ دليل على
And the presence of a dead animal in the well makes it impure from one day and night (according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as a precaution) and if it is bloated, it makes it impure from three days and nights, if the time of its fall is not known. Because bloating indicates that time has passed, so prayers of that period must be repeated if they performed wudu or ghusl from it while in a state of impurity. Commentary: The restriction to 'animal' is because other impurities do not have this detail; they make the well impure from the time of finding only. The animal meant is with flowing blood, not aquatic. 'Bloated' includes hair falling out or decomposition. 'If time of fall is not known' - the condition is not knowing; if known or thought, there is no problem, and the ruling is from that time. Because bloating indicates passage of time, and the minimum for bloating is three days, as can be seen in funeral prayer over a buried person up to three days.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage deals with the specific case of a well from which people have been using water, and then a dead animal is found in it. If the time of the animal's death is unknown, the ruling depends on the animal's state: if it is not bloated, the impurity is considered to have been present for one day and one night (as a precaution from Imam Abu Hanifa). If it is bloated, the impurity is considered to have been present for three days and three nights. This means that any prayers and wudu performed using that water during that period must be repeated. The commentary clarifies that only animals with flowing blood (not aquatic animals) are considered here. If the animal is found with hair falling out or decomposed, the ruling is the same as bloated. The wisdom behind the three-day period is that bloating typically occurs after three days. An analogy is drawn with the permissibility of praying the funeral prayer on a grave up to three days after burial. If the time of death is known or reasonably estimated, then the ruling applies from that time without any presumption.

Key Terms
الانتفاخ (al-intifakh): Bloating of a dead animal's body, used as evidence that the death occurred at least three days ago.
تقادم العهد (taqadum al-'ahd): The passage of time; bloating indicates that the death occurred some time ago.
حيوان دموي (hayawan damawi): A land animal with flowing blood; aquatic or insects are excluded from this ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A dead, non-bloated animal is found in a well. When is the well considered to have become impure?
A) From the time of finding the animal.
B) From one day and one night prior to finding it.
C) From three days and three nights prior.
D) It is not considered impure because it is not bloated.

Key Takeaway
When a dead land animal is found in a well and the time of death is unknown, impurity is presumed retroactively based on bloating: one day if not bloated, three days if bloated; all prayers of that period must be repeated.

Dispute on Timing of Well Impurity: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the timing of well impurity from a dead animal and apply the tarjih to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Bloating indicates three days; but certainty is not removed by doubt—the Sahibayn's view prevails.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when exactly does a dead animal found in a well render its water impure? Imam Abu Hanifa uses physical signs like bloating to estimate the moment of death, while his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, insist on certainty at the time of discovery. The commentary by al-Tahtawi traces the arguments and ultimately adopts the Sahibayn's position as the sound one, illustrating how later Hanafi scholars resolved this practical problem. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate both views, understand the evidence they rely on, and apply the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the exact contested point: the timing from which the well water is considered impure when a dead animal is found but the time of death is unknown.
The issue is not whether the water is impure, but when the impurity began.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and evidence
Imam holds that if the animal is not bloated, impurity dates back one day and night; if bloated, three days and nights. Bloated is taken as a reliable indication of three days' lapse.
Bloating (al-intifākh) is a legal indicator (dalīl al-taqādum) based on common experience.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and evidence
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that impurity begins only from the time of discovery, since the exact time of death is unknown. Certainty (the original state of purity) is not removed by doubt.
This is supported by the story of Abu Yusuf witnessing a kite drop a carcass, implying death may have just occurred.
4
Analyse the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Imam's view obligates repetition of prayers performed with that water during the presumed period; the Sahibayn's view does not require any repetition.
The difference has a direct impact on people's obligations.
5
Apply the tarjih (preponderant view)
Al-Tahtawi states that the Sahibayn's position is al-sahih (the sound one). In later fatwa practice, the Sahibayn's view is the muftā bihī.
This illustrates the process of tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجود حیوان ميت فيها أي البئر ينجسها من يوم وليلة عند الإمام احتياطاً ومنتفخ ينجسها من ثلاثة أيام ولياليها إن لم يعلم وقت وقوعه لأن الانتفاخ دليل تقادم العهد فيلزم إعادة صلوات تلك المدة إذا توضؤا منها وهم محدثون أو اغتسلوا من جنابة وإن كانوا متوضئين، أو غسلوا الثياب لا عن نجاسة، فلا إعادة إجماعاً وإن غسلوا الثياب من نجاسة، ولم يتوضؤا منها، فلا يلزمهم إلا غسلها في الصحيح لأنه من قبيل وجود النجاسة في الثوب، ولم يدر وقت إصابتها، ولا يعيد صلاته اتفاقاً هو الصحيح وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد يحكم بنجاستها، من وقت العلم بها ولا يلزمهم إعادة شيء من الصلوات، ولا غسل ما أصابه ماؤها في الزمان الماضي حتى يتحققوا متى وقعت فإن عجن الآن بمائها قيل يلقى للكلاب، أو يعلف به المواشي، وقال بعضهم يباع لشافعي وإن وجد بثوبه منيا أعاد من آخر نومة، وفي الدم لا يعيد شيئاً لأنه يصيبه من الخارج. قرب عهده فقدر بيوم وليلة لأن ما دون ذلك ساعات لا تنضبط، ومثله نجاسة الثياب، ووقع الشك في المزيل واليقين لا يزول بالشك. قوله : فلا إعادة إجماعاً لوجود المقتضى للصحة، وهو الطهارة من الحدث والخبث ووقع الشك في المانع، وهو إصابة ذلك الماء والصلاة لا تبطل بالشك. قوله : ولا يعيد صلاته اتفاقاً لا يتجه على قول الإمام لأن قياسه أن يوجب مع الغسل الإعادة، ولا على قولهما لأنهما لا يوجبان غسل الثياب أصلاً. قوله: وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد يحكم بنجاستها من وقت العلم لجواز أنه سقط فيها فمات في الحال، أو ألقته الريح، أو بعض السفهاء، أو الصبيان، أو الطيور حكي عن أبي يوسف أنه قال : كان قولي كقول الإمام إلى أن كنت جالساً في بستان، فرأيت حدأة في منقارها جيفة فطرحتها في البئر، فرجعت إلى قول محمد قوله : فإن عجن الآن بمائها أي بعد العلم بالنجاسة. قوله : يباع لشافعي لأن الماء إذا بلغ قلتين لا ينجس عنده بدون ظهور أثر . قوله: لأنه يصيبه من الخارج بخلاف المني حتى أن الثوب إن كان مما يلبسه هو وغيره يستوي فيه حكم الدم والمني قال البرهان الحلبي : الحكم بالاقتصار فيما لو رأى على ثوبه نجاسة إنما يتأتى في الرطبة، أما اليابسة فينبغي أن يتحرى وقت إصابتها عنده وكذا عندهما إذ لا يتأتى أن يقال إنها إصابته تلك الساعة بعد يبسها إلا أن يكون الزمان محتملاً ليبسها بعد الأصابة، وهو تفصيل حسن
And the presence of a dead animal in it, i.e., the well, renders it impure from the day and night according to Imam Abu Hanifa as a precaution, and [if] bloated, it renders it impure from three days and their nights if the time of its fall is not known, because bloating is evidence of lapse of time. So it becomes necessary to repeat the prayers of that period if they performed wudu from it while in a state of hadath or performed ghusl from janabah, even if they were in a state of wudu, or washed clothes not due to impurity; then there is no repetition by consensus. And if they washed clothes due to impurity and did not perform wudu from it, then only washing the clothes is necessary according to the sound position, because it is of the category of finding impurity on the garment and not knowing the time of its occurrence, and he does not repeat his prayer by agreement; that is the sound position. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: it is ruled impure from the time it is known, and they are not obligated to repeat any prayers or wash what its water touched in the past time until they ascertain when it fell. Then if dough is now made with its water, it is said to be thrown to dogs or fed to livestock, and some said it is sold to a Shafi'i. And if he finds semen on his garment, he repeats from the last sleep; for blood, he does not repeat anything because it comes from outside. Its closeness of time is estimated by a day and night because less than that are hours that cannot be specified, and similarly the impurity of garments, and doubt arises about the remover, and certainty is not removed by doubt. He said: So no repetition by consensus because the basis for validity exists, which is purification from hadath and impurity, and doubt arose about the impediment, which is that water's contact, and prayer is not invalidated by doubt. He said: And he does not repeat his prayer by agreement; it is not consistent with Imam's view because his analogy would require repetition along with washing, nor with their view because they do not require washing garments at all. He said: And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad ruled it impure from the time of knowledge because it is possible that it fell and died immediately, or the wind tossed it, or some foolish people, or children, or birds. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he said: My view was like Imam's until I was sitting in a garden and saw a kite with a carcass in its beak which it threw into the well, so I reverted to Muhammad's view. He said: If dough is now made with its water, i.e., after knowledge of impurity. He said: It is sold to a Shafi'i because water does not become impure in his view if it reaches two qullahs without manifest trace. He said: Because it comes from outside, unlike semen, so that if the garment is worn by him and others, the ruling of blood and semen is equal. Al-Burhan al-Halabi said: The ruling of restricting [to the last sleep] when he sees impurity on his garment only applies to wet [impurity]; as for dry, he should try to determine the time of its occurrence according to Abu Hanifa and also according to them two, because it is not possible to say that it affected him at that very hour after it dried unless the time allows for its drying after occurrence, and this is a good detail.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the rulings concerning a dead animal found in a well. The author presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the timing of the impurity. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the dead animal is not bloated, it renders the water impure from the time of finding back one day and night; if bloated, three days and nights, as a precautionary measure. The bloating is taken as evidence that the animal died three days earlier, as that is the typical time for bloating to appear. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the water is impure only from the time the animal is discovered, because the exact time of death is unknown. The passage then discusses the implications: if people performed wudu or ghusl from that water during the presumed period, Abu Hanifa requires them to repeat their prayers (since they were in a state of impurity unknowingly), while the Sahibayn do not require repetition. The text also addresses a related issue: if a person finds blood on his garment, he does not repeat prayers unless it is semen, in which case he repeats from the last sleep, as blood comes from outside (common occurrences) while semen is specific to oneself. The commentary also includes a story of Abu Yusuf changing his view after seeing a kite drop a carcass into a well, illustrating the shift to the Sahibayn's position. Finally, a detail from al-Burhan al-Halabi distinguishes between wet and dry impurities on clothes: for wet, one can assume recent contact; for dry, one must try to estimate the time.

Key Terms
الانتفاخ (al-intifākh): Bloating of a dead animal indicating death occurred about three days prior
تقادم العهد (taqādum al-ʿahd): Lapse of time since death; deduced from physical signs like bloating
نزح (nazḥ): Drawing water from a well to remove impurity

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) From three days before discovery if bloated

Key Takeaway
When a dead animal is found in a well, the Hanafi mufta bihi holds the water becomes impure only from the time of discovery; no prayer repetition is required unless the time of death is certain.
